<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Coca-Cola</h1>			<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
				<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>				<div id="contentSub"></div>
				<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>				<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
				<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
				<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">This article is about the beverage. For its manufacturer, see <a href="/wiki/The_Coca-Cola_Company" title="The Coca-Cola Company">The Coca-Cola Company</a>.</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">"Coca-Cola Classic" redirects here. For the college football game, see <a href="/wiki/Coca-Cola_Classic_(college_football)" title="Coca-Cola Classic (college football)">Coca-Cola Classic (college football)</a>.</div>

<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:15-09-26-RalfR-WLC-0098.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/15-09-26-RalfR-WLC-0098.jpg/220px-15-09-26-RalfR-WLC-0098.jpg" width="220" height="390" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/15-09-26-RalfR-WLC-0098.jpg/330px-15-09-26-RalfR-WLC-0098.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/15-09-26-RalfR-WLC-0098.jpg/440px-15-09-26-RalfR-WLC-0098.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2354" data-file-height="4174" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:15-09-26-RalfR-WLC-0098.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Coca-Cola bottle</div></div></div>
<p><b>Coca-Cola</b>, or <b>Coke</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Carbonation" title="Carbonation">carbonated</a> <a href="/wiki/Soft_drink" title="Soft drink">soft drink</a><sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> manufactured by <a href="/wiki/The_Coca-Cola_Company" title="The Coca-Cola Company">The Coca-Cola Company</a>. Originally intended as a <a href="/wiki/Patent_medicine" title="Patent medicine">patent medicine</a>, it was invented in the late 19th century by <a href="/wiki/John_Pemberton" title="John Pemberton">John Pemberton</a> and was bought out by businessman <a href="/wiki/Asa_Griggs_Candler" title="Asa Griggs Candler">Asa Griggs Candler</a>, whose marketing tactics led Coca-Cola to its dominance of the world soft-drink market throughout the 20th century. The drink's name refers to two of its original ingredients: <a href="/wiki/Coca" title="Coca">coca</a> leaves, and <a href="/wiki/Kola_nut" title="Kola nut">kola nuts</a> (a source of <a href="/wiki/Caffeine" title="Caffeine">caffeine</a>). The current <a href="/wiki/Coca-Cola_formula" title="Coca-Cola formula">formula of Coca-Cola</a> remains a <a href="/wiki/Trade_secret" title="Trade secret">trade secret</a>, although a variety of reported recipes and experimental recreations have been published.
</p><p>The Coca-Cola Company produces <a href="/wiki/Concentrate" title="Concentrate">concentrate</a>, which is then sold to licensed Coca-Cola bottlers throughout the world. The bottlers, who hold exclusive territory contracts with the company, produce the finished product in cans and bottles from the concentrate, in combination with filtered water and sweeteners. A typical 12-US-fluid-ounce (350&#160;ml) can contains 38 grams (1.3&#160;oz) of sugar (usually in the form of <a href="/wiki/High_fructose_corn_syrup" class="mw-redirect" title="High fructose corn syrup">high fructose corn syrup</a>). The bottlers then sell, distribute, and merchandise Coca-Cola to retail stores, restaurants, and <a href="/wiki/Vending_machine" title="Vending machine">vending machines</a> throughout the world. The Coca-Cola Company also sells concentrate for <a href="/wiki/Soda_fountain" title="Soda fountain">soda fountains</a> of major restaurants and <a href="/wiki/Foodservice" title="Foodservice">foodservice</a> distributors.
</p><p>The Coca-Cola Company has on occasion introduced other cola drinks under the Coke name. The most common of these is <a href="/wiki/Diet_Coke" title="Diet Coke">Diet Coke</a>, along with others including <a href="/wiki/Caffeine-Free_Coca-Cola" title="Caffeine-Free Coca-Cola">Caffeine-Free Coca-Cola</a>, <a href="/wiki/Diet_Coke_Caffeine-Free" class="mw-redirect" title="Diet Coke Caffeine-Free">Diet Coke Caffeine-Free</a>, <a href="/wiki/Coca-Cola_Zero_Sugar" title="Coca-Cola Zero Sugar">Coca-Cola Zero Sugar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Coca-Cola_Cherry" title="Coca-Cola Cherry">Coca-Cola Cherry</a>, <a href="/wiki/Coca-Cola_Vanilla" title="Coca-Cola Vanilla">Coca-Cola Vanilla</a>, and special versions with <a href="/wiki/Coca-Cola_with_Lemon" title="Coca-Cola with Lemon">lemon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Coca-Cola_with_Lime" title="Coca-Cola with Lime">lime</a>, and coffee. Based on <a href="/wiki/Interbrand" title="Interbrand">Interbrand</a>'s "best global brand" study of 2015, Coca-Cola was the world's third most valuable brand, after Apple and Google.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> In 2013, Coke products were sold in over 200 countries worldwide, with consumers drinking more than 1.8 billion company beverage servings each day.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="19th-century_historical_origins">19th-century historical origins</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Pemberton%27s_Eagle_and_Drug_House,_Birthplace_of_Coca-Cola.jpeg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/a9/Pemberton%27s_Eagle_and_Drug_House%2C_Birthplace_of_Coca-Cola.jpeg/220px-Pemberton%27s_Eagle_and_Drug_House%2C_Birthplace_of_Coca-Cola.jpeg" width="220" height="162" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/a9/Pemberton%27s_Eagle_and_Drug_House%2C_Birthplace_of_Coca-Cola.jpeg/330px-Pemberton%27s_Eagle_and_Drug_House%2C_Birthplace_of_Coca-Cola.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/a/a9/Pemberton%27s_Eagle_and_Drug_House%2C_Birthplace_of_Coca-Cola.jpeg 2x" data-file-width="351" data-file-height="259" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Pemberton%27s_Eagle_and_Drug_House,_Birthplace_of_Coca-Cola.jpeg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Eagle Drug and Chemical House in Columbus, Georgia</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:John_Pemberton.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/John_Pemberton.jpg/220px-John_Pemberton.jpg" width="220" height="272" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/John_Pemberton.jpg/330px-John_Pemberton.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/John_Pemberton.jpg/440px-John_Pemberton.jpg 2x" data-file-width="485" data-file-height="600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:John_Pemberton.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/John_Pemberton" title="John Pemberton">John Pemberton</a>, the original inventor of Coca-Cola</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:19th_century_Coca-Cola_coupon.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/19th_century_Coca-Cola_coupon.jpg/220px-19th_century_Coca-Cola_coupon.jpg" width="220" height="110" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/19th_century_Coca-Cola_coupon.jpg/330px-19th_century_Coca-Cola_coupon.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/19th_century_Coca-Cola_coupon.jpg/440px-19th_century_Coca-Cola_coupon.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1295" data-file-height="646" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:19th_century_Coca-Cola_coupon.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Believed to be the first coupon ever, this ticket for a free glass of Coca-Cola was first distributed in 1888 to help promote the drink. By 1913, the company had redeemed 8.5 million tickets.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Coca_Cola_ad_ca._1943_IMG_3744.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Coca_Cola_ad_ca._1943_IMG_3744.JPG/220px-Coca_Cola_ad_ca._1943_IMG_3744.JPG" width="220" height="234" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Coca_Cola_ad_ca._1943_IMG_3744.JPG/330px-Coca_Cola_ad_ca._1943_IMG_3744.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Coca_Cola_ad_ca._1943_IMG_3744.JPG/440px-Coca_Cola_ad_ca._1943_IMG_3744.JPG 2x" data-file-width="4000" data-file-height="4250" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Coca_Cola_ad_ca._1943_IMG_3744.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>This Coca-Cola advertisement from 1943 is still displayed in <a href="/wiki/Minden,_Louisiana" title="Minden, Louisiana">Minden</a>, Louisiana.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Coca_Cola_Bottling_Machine,_Biedenharn_Museum_and_Gardens_IMG_4101.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Coca_Cola_Bottling_Machine%2C_Biedenharn_Museum_and_Gardens_IMG_4101.JPG/220px-Coca_Cola_Bottling_Machine%2C_Biedenharn_Museum_and_Gardens_IMG_4101.JPG" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Coca_Cola_Bottling_Machine%2C_Biedenharn_Museum_and_Gardens_IMG_4101.JPG/330px-Coca_Cola_Bottling_Machine%2C_Biedenharn_Museum_and_Gardens_IMG_4101.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Coca_Cola_Bottling_Machine%2C_Biedenharn_Museum_and_Gardens_IMG_4101.JPG/440px-Coca_Cola_Bottling_Machine%2C_Biedenharn_Museum_and_Gardens_IMG_4101.JPG 2x" data-file-width="4272" data-file-height="2848" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Coca_Cola_Bottling_Machine,_Biedenharn_Museum_and_Gardens_IMG_4101.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Early Coca-Cola vending machine at <a href="/wiki/Biedenharn_Museum_and_Gardens" title="Biedenharn Museum and Gardens">Biedenharn Museum and Gardens</a> in <a href="/wiki/Monroe,_Louisiana" title="Monroe, Louisiana">Monroe</a>, Louisiana</div></div></div>
<p>Confederate Colonel <a href="/wiki/John_Pemberton" title="John Pemberton">John Pemberton</a>, who was wounded in the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">American Civil War</a> and became addicted to <a href="/wiki/Morphine" title="Morphine">morphine</a>, began a quest to find a substitute for the problematic drug.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> The prototype <a href="/wiki/Coca-Cola_formula" title="Coca-Cola formula">Coca-Cola recipe</a> was formulated at Pemberton's Eagle Drug and Chemical House,<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> a drugstore in <a href="/wiki/Columbus,_Georgia" title="Columbus, Georgia">Columbus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(U.S._state)" title="Georgia (U.S. state)">Georgia</a>, originally as a <a href="/wiki/Coca_wine" title="Coca wine">coca wine</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> He may have been inspired by the formidable success of <a href="/wiki/Vin_Mariani" title="Vin Mariani">Vin Mariani</a>, a French-<a href="/wiki/Corsica" title="Corsica">Corsican</a> coca wine.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> It is also worth noting that a Spanish drink called "Kola Coca" was presented at a contest in Philadelphia in 1885, a year before the official birth of Coca-Cola. The rights for this Spanish drink were bought by Coca-Cola in 1953.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1885, Pemberton registered his <a href="/wiki/Pemberton%27s_French_Wine_Coca" title="Pemberton&#39;s French Wine Coca">French Wine Coca</a> nerve tonic.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> In 1886, when Atlanta and <a href="/wiki/Fulton_County,_Georgia" title="Fulton County, Georgia">Fulton County</a> passed <a href="/wiki/Prohibition" title="Prohibition">prohibition</a> legislation, Pemberton responded by developing Coca-Cola, a nonalcoholic version of <a href="/wiki/Pemberton%27s_French_Wine_Coca" title="Pemberton&#39;s French Wine Coca">French Wine Coca</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>
The first sales were at Jacob's Pharmacy in Atlanta, Georgia, on May 8, 1886.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> It was initially sold as a <a href="/wiki/Patent_medicine" title="Patent medicine">patent medicine</a> for five <a href="/wiki/Cent_(currency)" title="Cent (currency)">cents</a><sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> a glass at <a href="/wiki/Soda_fountain" title="Soda fountain">soda fountains</a>, which were popular in the United States at the time due to the belief that <a href="/wiki/Carbonated_water" title="Carbonated water">carbonated water</a> was good for the health.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> Pemberton claimed Coca-Cola cured many diseases, including morphine addiction, indigestion, nerve disorders, headaches, and <a href="/wiki/Impotence" class="mw-redirect" title="Impotence">impotence</a>. Pemberton ran the first advertisement for the beverage on May 29 of the same year in the <i><a href="/wiki/Atlanta_Journal" class="mw-redirect" title="Atlanta Journal">Atlanta Journal</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>By 1888, three versions of Coca-Cola – sold by three separate businesses – were on the market. A co-partnership had been formed on January 14, 1888 between Pemberton and four Atlanta businessmen: J.C. Mayfield, A.O. Murphey, C.O. Mullahy, and E.H. Bloodworth. Not codified by any signed document, a verbal statement given by Asa Candler years later asserted under testimony that he had acquired a stake in Pemberton's company as early as 1887.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> John Pemberton declared that the <i>name</i> "Coca-Cola" belonged to his son, Charley, but the other two manufacturers could continue to use the <i>formula</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Pendergrast1_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pendergrast1-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Charley Pemberton's record of control over the "Coca-Cola" name was the underlying factor that allowed for him to participate as a major shareholder in the March 1888 Coca-Cola Company incorporation filing made in his father's place.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> Charley's exclusive control over the "Coca-Cola" name became a continual thorn in Asa Candler's side.
Candler's oldest son, Charles Howard Candler, authored a book in 1950 published by <a href="/wiki/Emory_University" title="Emory University">Emory University</a>. In this definitive biography about his father, Candler specifically states: "..., on April 14, 1888, the young druggist Asa Griggs Candler purchased a one-third interest in the formula of an almost completely unknown proprietary elixir known as Coca-Cola."<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:-_Coca-Cola_-_Old_bottle_opener_-.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/-_Coca-Cola_-_Old_bottle_opener_-.jpg/220px--_Coca-Cola_-_Old_bottle_opener_-.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/-_Coca-Cola_-_Old_bottle_opener_-.jpg/330px--_Coca-Cola_-_Old_bottle_opener_-.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/-_Coca-Cola_-_Old_bottle_opener_-.jpg/440px--_Coca-Cola_-_Old_bottle_opener_-.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3072" data-file-height="2048" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:-_Coca-Cola_-_Old_bottle_opener_-.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Old German Coca-Cola <a href="/wiki/Bottle_opener" title="Bottle opener">bottle opener</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The deal was actually between John Pemberton's son Charley and Walker, Candler &amp; Co. – with John Pemberton acting as cosigner for his son. For $50 down and $500 in 30 days, Walker, Candler &amp; Co. obtained all of the one-third interest in the Coca-Cola Company that Charley held, all while Charley still held on to the name. After the April 14 deal, on April 17, 1888, one-half of the Walker/Dozier interest shares were acquired by Candler for an additional $750.<sup id="cite_ref-ForGodCountryandCocaColapg44-45_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ForGodCountryandCocaColapg44-45-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Coca-Cola_Company">The Coca-Cola Company</span></h3>
<p>In 1892, Candler set out to incorporate a second company; "The Coca-Cola Company" (the current corporation). When Candler had the earliest records of the "Coca-Cola Company" burned in 1910, the action was claimed to have been made during a move to new corporation offices around this time.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>After Candler had gained a better foothold on Coca-Cola in April 1888, he nevertheless was forced to sell the beverage he produced with the recipe he had under the names "Yum Yum" and "Koke". This was while Charley Pemberton was selling the elixir, although a cruder mixture, under the name "Coca-Cola", all with his father's blessing. After both names failed to catch on for Candler, by the middle of 1888, the Atlanta pharmacist was quite anxious to establish a firmer legal claim to Coca-Cola, and hoped he could force his two competitors, Walker and Dozier, completely out of the business, as well.<sup id="cite_ref-ForGodCountryandCocaColapg44-45_21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ForGodCountryandCocaColapg44-45-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On August 16, 1888, Dr. John Stith Pemberton suddenly died; Asa G. Candler then sought to move swiftly forward to attain his vision of taking full control of the whole Coca-Cola operation.
</p><p>Charley Pemberton, an alcoholic, was the one obstacle who unnerved Asa Candler more than anyone else. Candler is said to have quickly maneuvered to purchase the exclusive rights to the name "Coca-Cola" from Pemberton's son Charley right after Dr. Pemberton's death. One of several stories was that Candler bought the title to the name from Charley's mother for $300; approaching her at Dr. Pemberton's funeral. Eventually, Charley Pemberton was found on June 23, 1894, unconscious, with a stick of opium by his side. Ten days later, Charley died at Atlanta's Grady Hospital at the age of 40.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In Charles Howard Candler's 1950 book about his father, he stated: "On August 30th [1888], he [Asa Candler] became sole proprietor of Coca-Cola, a fact which was stated on letterheads, invoice blanks and advertising copy."<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>With this action on August 30, 1888, Candler's sole control became technically all true. Candler had negotiated with Margaret Dozier and her brother Woolfolk Walker a full payment amounting to $1,000, which all agreed Candler could pay off with a series of notes over a specified time span. By May 1, 1889, Candler was now claiming full ownership of the Coca-Cola beverage, with a total investment outlay by Candler for the drink enterprise over the years amounting to $2,300.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1914, Margaret Dozier, as co-owner of the original Coca-Cola Company in 1888, came forward to claim that her signature on the 1888 Coca-Cola Company bill of sale had been forged. Subsequent analysis of certain similar transfer documents had also indicated John Pemberton's signature was most likely a forgery, as well, which some accounts claim was precipitated by his son Charley.<sup id="cite_ref-Pendergrast1_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pendergrast1-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On September 12, 1919, Coca-Cola Co. was purchased by a group of investors for $25 million and reincorporated. The company publicly offered 500,000 shares of the company for $40 a share.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1986, The Coca-Cola Company merged with two of their bottling operators (owned by JTL Corporation and BCI Holding Corporation) to form Coca-Cola Enterprises Inc. (CCE).<sup id="cite_ref-cokecce.com_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cokecce.com-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In December 1991, Coca-Cola Enterprises merged with the Johnston Coca-Cola Bottling Group, Inc.<sup id="cite_ref-cokecce.com_28-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cokecce.com-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Origins_of_bottling">Origins of bottling</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Commercial._At_the_Coca_Cola_Plant_BAnQ_P48S1P06539.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Commercial._At_the_Coca_Cola_Plant_BAnQ_P48S1P06539.jpg/220px-Commercial._At_the_Coca_Cola_Plant_BAnQ_P48S1P06539.jpg" width="220" height="140" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Commercial._At_the_Coca_Cola_Plant_BAnQ_P48S1P06539.jpg/330px-Commercial._At_the_Coca_Cola_Plant_BAnQ_P48S1P06539.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Commercial._At_the_Coca_Cola_Plant_BAnQ_P48S1P06539.jpg/440px-Commercial._At_the_Coca_Cola_Plant_BAnQ_P48S1P06539.jpg 2x" data-file-width="960" data-file-height="611" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Commercial._At_the_Coca_Cola_Plant_BAnQ_P48S1P06539.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bottling plant of Coca-Cola Canada Ltd. January 8, 1941. <a href="/wiki/Montreal" title="Montreal">Montreal</a>, Canada.</div></div></div>
<p>The first bottling of Coca-Cola occurred in <a href="/wiki/Vicksburg,_Mississippi" title="Vicksburg, Mississippi">Vicksburg</a>, Mississippi, at the Biedenharn Candy Company in 1894.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> The proprietor of the bottling works was <a href="/wiki/Joseph_A._Biedenharn" title="Joseph A. Biedenharn">Joseph A. Biedenharn</a>. The original bottles were <a href="/wiki/Hutchinson_Patent_Stopper" title="Hutchinson Patent Stopper">Hutchinson</a> bottles, very different from the much later hobble-skirt design of 1915 now so familiar.
</p><p>It was then a few years later that two entrepreneurs from <a href="/wiki/Chattanooga,_Tennessee" title="Chattanooga, Tennessee">Chattanooga, Tennessee</a>, namely <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Thomas_(Coca-Cola_bottler)" class="mw-redirect" title="Benjamin Thomas (Coca-Cola bottler)">Benjamin F. Thomas</a> and <a href="/wiki/Joseph_B._Whitehead" class="mw-redirect" title="Joseph B. Whitehead">Joseph B. Whitehead</a>, proposed the idea of bottling and were so persuasive that Candler signed a contract giving them control of the procedure for only one dollar.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> Candler never collected his dollar, but in 1899, Chattanooga became the site of the first Coca-Cola bottling company. Candler remained very content just selling his company's syrup.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> The loosely termed contract proved to be problematic for The Coca-Cola Company for decades to come. Legal matters were not helped by the decision of the bottlers to subcontract to other companies, effectively becoming parent bottlers.<sup id="cite_ref-history-of-bottling_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-history-of-bottling-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> This contract specified that bottles would be sold at 5¢ each and had no fixed duration, leading to <a href="/wiki/The_fixed_price_of_Coca-Cola_from_1886_to_1959" class="mw-redirect" title="The fixed price of Coca-Cola from 1886 to 1959">the fixed price of Coca-Cola from 1886 to 1959</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="20th_century">20th century</span></h3>
<p>The first outdoor wall advertisement that promoted the Coca-Cola drink was painted in 1894 in <a href="/wiki/Cartersville,_Georgia" title="Cartersville, Georgia">Cartersville, Georgia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>  Cola syrup was sold as an over-the-counter <a href="/wiki/Dietary_supplement" title="Dietary supplement">dietary supplement</a> for upset stomach.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>  By the time of its 50th anniversary, the soft drink had reached the status of a national icon in the USA. In 1935, it was certified <a href="/wiki/Kosher" class="mw-redirect" title="Kosher">kosher</a> by Atlanta Rabbi <a href="/wiki/Tobias_Geffen" title="Tobias Geffen">Tobias Geffen</a>, after the company made minor changes in the sourcing of some ingredients.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:1BILLIONTHgallonCOCACOLAowner.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/1BILLIONTHgallonCOCACOLAowner.jpg/220px-1BILLIONTHgallonCOCACOLAowner.jpg" width="220" height="273" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/1BILLIONTHgallonCOCACOLAowner.jpg/330px-1BILLIONTHgallonCOCACOLAowner.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/1BILLIONTHgallonCOCACOLAowner.jpg/440px-1BILLIONTHgallonCOCACOLAowner.jpg 2x" data-file-width="744" data-file-height="924" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:1BILLIONTHgallonCOCACOLAowner.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Original framed Coca-Cola artist's drawn graphic presented by The Coca-Cola Company on July 12, 1944 to Charles Howard Candler on the occasion of Coca-Cola's "1 Billionth Gallon of Coca-Cola Syrup."</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:FLEEMANSpharmacyCOKErwLIPACKowner.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/FLEEMANSpharmacyCOKErwLIPACKowner.jpg/220px-FLEEMANSpharmacyCOKErwLIPACKowner.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/FLEEMANSpharmacyCOKErwLIPACKowner.jpg/330px-FLEEMANSpharmacyCOKErwLIPACKowner.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/FLEEMANSpharmacyCOKErwLIPACKowner.jpg/440px-FLEEMANSpharmacyCOKErwLIPACKowner.jpg 2x" data-file-width="792" data-file-height="528" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:FLEEMANSpharmacyCOKErwLIPACKowner.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Claimed to be the first installation anywhere of the 1948 model "Boat Motor" styled Coca-Cola soda dispenser, Fleeman's Pharmacy, Atlanta, Georgia. The "Boat Motor" soda dispenser was introduced in the late 1930s and manufactured till the late 1950s. Photograph circa 1948.</div></div></div>
<p>The longest running commercial Coca-Cola soda fountain anywhere was Atlanta's Fleeman's Pharmacy, which first opened its doors in 1914.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> Jack Fleeman took over the pharmacy from his father and ran it until 1995; closing it after 81 years.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>  On July 12, 1944, the one-billionth gallon of Coca-Cola syrup was manufactured by The Coca-Cola Company.  Cans of Coke first appeared in 1955.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="New_Coke">New Coke</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/New_Coke" title="New Coke">New Coke</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:World-of-coca-cola.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/World-of-coca-cola.jpg/220px-World-of-coca-cola.jpg" width="220" height="299" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/World-of-coca-cola.jpg/330px-World-of-coca-cola.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/World-of-coca-cola.jpg/440px-World-of-coca-cola.jpg 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="816" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:World-of-coca-cola.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Las_Vegas_Strip" title="Las Vegas Strip">Las Vegas Strip</a> <i><a href="/wiki/World_of_Coca-Cola" title="World of Coca-Cola">World of Coca-Cola</a></i> museum in 2003</div></div></div>
<p>On April 23, 1985, Coca-Cola, amid much publicity, attempted to change the <a href="/wiki/Coca-Cola_formula" title="Coca-Cola formula">formula</a> of the drink with "New Coke". Follow-up taste tests revealed most consumers preferred the taste of New Coke to both Coke and <a href="/wiki/Pepsi" title="Pepsi">Pepsi</a><sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> but Coca-Cola management was unprepared for the public's <a href="/wiki/Nostalgia" title="Nostalgia">nostalgia</a> for the old drink, leading to a <a href="/wiki/Backlash_(sociology)" title="Backlash (sociology)">backlash</a>. The company gave in to protests and returned to a variation of the old formula using high fructose corn syrup instead of cane sugar as the main sweetener, under the name Coca-Cola Classic, on July 10, 1985.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="21st_century">21st century</span></h3>
<p>On July 5, 2005, it was revealed that Coca-Cola would resume operations in Iraq for the first time since the <a href="/wiki/Arab_League" title="Arab League">Arab League</a> boycotted the company in 1968.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In April 2007, in Canada, the name "Coca-Cola Classic" was changed back to "Coca-Cola". The word "Classic" was removed because "New Coke" was no longer in production, eliminating the need to differentiate between the two.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> The formula remained unchanged. In January 2009, Coca-Cola stopped printing the word "Classic" on the labels of 16-US-fluid-ounce (470&#160;ml) bottles sold in parts of the southeastern United States.<sup id="cite_ref-wsj_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wsj-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> The change is part of a larger strategy to rejuvenate the product's image.<sup id="cite_ref-wsj_43-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wsj-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> The word "Classic" was removed from all Coca-Cola products by 2011.
</p><p>In November 2009, due to a dispute over wholesale prices of Coca-Cola products, <a href="/wiki/Costco" title="Costco">Costco</a> stopped restocking its shelves with Coke and Diet Coke for two months; a separate pouring rights deal in 2013 saw Coke products removed from Costco food courts in favor of Pepsi.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> Some Costco locations (such as the ones in <a href="/wiki/Tucson,_Arizona" title="Tucson, Arizona">Tucson, Arizona</a>) additionally sell <a href="/wiki/Mexican_Coke" title="Mexican Coke">imported Coca-Cola from Mexico</a> with cane sugar instead of corn syrup from separate distributors.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>  Coca-Cola introduced the 7.5-ounce mini-can in 2009, and on September 22, 2011, the company announced price reductions, asking retailers to sell eight-packs for $2.99. That same day, Coca-Cola announced the 12.5-ounce bottle, to sell for 89 cents. A 16-ounce bottle has sold well at 99 cents since being re-introduced, but the price was going up to $1.19.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>In 2012, Coca-Cola resumed business in Myanmar after 60 years of absence due to U.S.-imposed investment sanctions against the country.<sup id="cite_ref-BBC-GT-DEX-2012-CN-MP-X_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC-GT-DEX-2012-CN-MP-X-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BLOOMBERG-GT-DEX-2012-CN-MP-X_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BLOOMBERG-GT-DEX-2012-CN-MP-X-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> Coca-Cola's bottling plant will be located in <a href="/wiki/Yangon" title="Yangon">Yangon</a> and is part of the company's five-year plan and $200 million investment in Myanmar.<sup id="cite_ref-investvine_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-investvine-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> Coca-Cola with its partners is to invest USD 5 billion in its operations in India by 2020.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> In 2013, it was announced that <a href="/wiki/Coca-Cola_Life" title="Coca-Cola Life">Coca-Cola Life</a> would be introduced in Argentina that would contain <a href="/wiki/Stevia" title="Stevia">stevia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sugar" title="Sugar">sugar</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Geller_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Geller-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Production">Production</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ingredients">Ingredients</span></h3>
<ul><li>Carbonated water</li>
<li>Sugar (<a href="/wiki/Sucrose" title="Sucrose">sucrose</a> or <a href="/wiki/High-fructose_corn_syrup" title="High-fructose corn syrup">high-fructose corn syrup</a> (HFCS) depending on country of origin)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Caffeine" title="Caffeine">Caffeine</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Phosphoric_acid" title="Phosphoric acid">Phosphoric acid</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Caramel_color#Classification" title="Caramel color">Caramel color (E150d)</a></li>
<li>Natural flavorings<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>A typical can of Coca-Cola (12 fl ounces/355&#160;ml) contains 38 grams of sugar (usually in the form of HFCS),<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> 50&#160;mg of sodium, 0&#160;grams fat, 0&#160;grams potassium, and 140 calories.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>
On May 5, 2014, Coca-Cola said it is working to remove a controversial ingredient, <a href="/wiki/Brominated_vegetable_oil" title="Brominated vegetable oil">brominated vegetable oil</a>, from all of its drinks.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Formula_of_natural_flavorings">Formula of natural flavorings</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Coca-Cola_formula" title="Coca-Cola formula">Coca-Cola formula</a></div>
<p>The exact formula of Coca-Cola's natural flavorings (but not its other ingredients, which are listed on the side of the bottle or can) is a <a href="/wiki/Trade_secret" title="Trade secret">trade secret</a>. The original copy of the formula was held in <a href="/wiki/SunTrust_Bank" class="mw-redirect" title="SunTrust Bank">SunTrust Bank</a>'s main vault in Atlanta for 86 years. Its predecessor, the Trust Company, was the <a href="/wiki/Underwriter" class="mw-redirect" title="Underwriter">underwriter</a> for the Coca-Cola Company's <a href="/wiki/Initial_public_offering" title="Initial public offering">initial public offering</a> in 1919. On December 8, 2011, the original secret formula was moved from the vault at SunTrust Banks to a new vault containing the formula which will be on display for visitors to its <a href="/wiki/World_of_Coca-Cola" title="World of Coca-Cola">World of Coca-Cola</a> museum in downtown Atlanta.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Coke_Museum.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Coke_Museum.JPG/220px-Coke_Museum.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Coke_Museum.JPG/330px-Coke_Museum.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Coke_Museum.JPG/440px-Coke_Museum.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3072" data-file-height="2304" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Coke_Museum.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Coca-Cola Museum in Atlanta, Georgia</div></div></div>
<p>According to Snopes, a popular myth states that only two executives have access to the formula, with each executive having only half the formula.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> However, several sources state that while Coca-Cola does have a rule restricting access to only two executives, each knows the entire formula and others, in addition to the prescribed duo, have known the formulation process.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On February 11, 2011, <a href="/wiki/Ira_Glass" title="Ira Glass">Ira Glass</a> said on his <a href="/wiki/Public_Radio_International" title="Public Radio International">PRI</a> radio show, <i><a href="/wiki/This_American_Life" title="This American Life">This American Life</a></i>, that <i>TAL</i> staffers had found a recipe in "Everett Beal's Recipe Book", reproduced in the February 28, 1979, issue of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Atlanta_Journal-Constitution" title="The Atlanta Journal-Constitution">The Atlanta Journal-Constitution</a></i>, that they believed was either Pemberton's original formula for Coca-Cola, or a version that he made either before or after the product hit the market in 1886. The formula basically matched the one found in Pemberton's diary.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>  Coca-Cola archivist Phil Mooney acknowledged that the recipe "could ... be a precursor" to the formula used in the original 1886 product, but emphasized that Pemberton's original formula is not the same as the one used in the current product.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Use_of_stimulants_in_formula">Use of stimulants in formula</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Pembertoncokeanzeige.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Pembertoncokeanzeige.jpg/220px-Pembertoncokeanzeige.jpg" width="220" height="282" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Pembertoncokeanzeige.jpg/330px-Pembertoncokeanzeige.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/f8/Pembertoncokeanzeige.jpg 2x" data-file-width="385" data-file-height="494" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Pembertoncokeanzeige.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An early Coca-Cola advertisement.</div></div></div>
<p>When launched, Coca-Cola's two key ingredients were <a href="/wiki/Cocaine" title="Cocaine">cocaine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Caffeine" title="Caffeine">caffeine</a>. The cocaine was derived from the <a href="/wiki/Coca" title="Coca">coca</a> leaf and the caffeine from <a href="/wiki/Kola_nut" title="Kola nut">kola nut</a> (also spelled "cola nut" at the time), leading to the name Coca-Cola.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Coca_.E2.80.93_cocaine"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Coca_–_cocaine">Coca – cocaine</span></h4>
<p>Pemberton called for five <a href="/wiki/Ounce" title="Ounce">ounces</a> of coca leaf per gallon of syrup (approximately 37&#160;g/L), a significant dose; in 1891, Candler claimed his formula (altered extensively from Pemberton's original) contained only a tenth of this amount. Coca-Cola once contained an estimated nine milligrams of cocaine per glass. (For comparison, a typical dose or "line" of cocaine is 50–75&#160;mg.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup>) In 1903, it was removed.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>After 1904, instead of using fresh leaves, Coca-Cola started using "spent" leaves&#160;– the leftovers of the cocaine-extraction process with trace levels of cocaine.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> Since then, Coca-Cola uses a cocaine-free coca leaf extract prepared at a <a href="/wiki/Stepan_Company" title="Stepan Company">Stepan Company</a> plant in <a href="/wiki/Maywood,_New_Jersey" title="Maywood, New Jersey">Maywood, New Jersey</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-mayclifford_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mayclifford-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the United States, the Stepan Company is the only manufacturing plant authorized by the Federal Government to import and process the coca plant,<sup id="cite_ref-mayclifford_67-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mayclifford-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> which it obtains mainly from Peru and, to a lesser extent, Bolivia. Besides producing the coca flavoring agent for Coca-Cola, the Stepan Company extracts cocaine from the coca leaves, which it sells to <a href="/wiki/Mallinckrodt" title="Mallinckrodt">Mallinckrodt</a>, a <a href="/wiki/St._Louis,_Missouri" class="mw-redirect" title="St. Louis, Missouri">St. Louis, Missouri</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pharmaceutical" class="mw-redirect" title="Pharmaceutical">pharmaceutical</a> manufacturer that is the only company in the United States licensed to purify cocaine for medicinal use.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Long after the syrup had ceased to contain any significant amount of cocaine, in the southeastern U.S., "dope" remained a common colloquialism for Coca-Cola, and "dope-wagons" were trucks that transported it.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> The traditional shape of the bottle is said to resemble the seed-pod of the coca bush, memorializing the cocaine recipe.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Kola_nuts_.E2.80.93_caffeine"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Kola_nuts_–_caffeine">Kola nuts – caffeine</span></h4>
<p>Kola nuts act as a flavoring and the source of caffeine in Coca-Cola. In Britain, for example, the ingredient label states "Flavourings (Including Caffeine)."<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> Kola nuts contain about 2.0 to 3.5% caffeine, are of bitter flavor, and are commonly used in <a href="/wiki/Cola" title="Cola">cola</a> <a href="/wiki/Soft_drinks" class="mw-redirect" title="Soft drinks">soft drinks</a>. In 1911, the U.S. government initiated <i><a href="/wiki/United_States_v._Forty_Barrels_and_Twenty_Kegs_of_Coca-Cola" class="mw-redirect" title="United States v. Forty Barrels and Twenty Kegs of Coca-Cola">United States v. Forty Barrels and Twenty Kegs of Coca-Cola</a></i>, hoping to force Coca-Cola to remove caffeine from its formula. The case was decided in favor of Coca-Cola. Subsequently, in 1912, the U.S. Pure Food and Drug Act was amended, adding caffeine to the list of "habit-forming" and "deleterious" substances which must be listed on a product's label.
</p><p>Coca-Cola contains 34&#160;mg of caffeine per 12 fluid ounces (9.8&#160;mg per 100&#160;ml).<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Franchised_production_model">Franchised production model</span></h3>
<p>The actual production and distribution of Coca-Cola follows a franchising model. The Coca-Cola Company only produces a syrup concentrate, which it sells to bottlers throughout the world, who hold Coca-Cola franchises for one or more geographical areas. The bottlers produce the final drink by mixing the syrup with filtered water and sweeteners, and then carbonate it before putting it in cans and bottles, which the bottlers then sell and distribute to retail stores, vending machines, restaurants, and food service distributors.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Coca-Cola Company owns minority shares in some of its largest franchises, such as <a href="/wiki/Coca-Cola_Enterprises" title="Coca-Cola Enterprises">Coca-Cola Enterprises</a>, <a href="/wiki/Coca-Cola_Amatil" title="Coca-Cola Amatil">Coca-Cola Amatil</a>, <a href="/wiki/Coca-Cola_Hellenic" class="mw-redirect" title="Coca-Cola Hellenic">Coca-Cola Hellenic</a> Bottling Company, and <a href="/wiki/Coca-Cola_FEMSA" title="Coca-Cola FEMSA">Coca-Cola FEMSA</a>, but fully independent bottlers produce almost half of the volume sold in the world.
Independent bottlers are allowed to sweeten the drink according to local tastes.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The bottling plant in <a href="/wiki/Skopje" title="Skopje">Skopje</a>, <a href="/wiki/Macedonia_(country)" class="mw-redirect" title="Macedonia (country)">Macedonia</a>, received the 2009 award for "Best Bottling Company".<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geographic_spread">Geographic spread</span></h2>
<p>Since it announced its intention to begin distribution in Myanmar in June 2012, Coca-Cola has been officially available in every country in the world except Cuba and North Korea.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> However, it is reported to be available in both countries as a <a href="/wiki/Grey_import" class="mw-redirect" title="Grey import">grey import</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Coca-Cola has been a point of legal discussion in the Middle East. In the early 20th century, a <a href="/wiki/Fatwa" title="Fatwa">fatwa</a> was created in Egypt to discuss the question of "whether Muslims were permitted to drink Coca-Cola and Pepsi cola."<sup id="cite_ref-Liebesny_1975_42–43_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Liebesny_1975_42–43-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> The fatwa states: "According to the Muslim Hanefite, Shafi'ite, etc., the rule in Islamic law of forbidding or allowing foods and beverages is based on the presumption that such things are permitted unless it can be shown that they are forbidden on the basis of the Qur'an."<sup id="cite_ref-Liebesny_1975_42–43_79-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Liebesny_1975_42–43-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> The Muslim jurists stated that, unless the Qu'ran specifically prohibits the consumption of a particular product, it is permissible to consume. Another clause was discussed, whereby the same rules apply if a person is unaware of the condition or ingredients of the item in question.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Brand_portfolio">Brand portfolio</span></h2>
<p>This is a list of variants of Coca-Cola introduced around the world. In addition to the caffeine-free version of the original, additional fruit flavors have been included over the years. Not included here are versions of <a href="/wiki/Diet_Coke" title="Diet Coke">Diet Coke</a> and <a href="/wiki/Coca-Cola_Zero" class="mw-redirect" title="Coca-Cola Zero">Coca-Cola Zero</a>; variant versions of those no-calorie colas can be found at their respective articles.
</p>
<ul><li><b><a href="/wiki/Caffeine-Free_Coca-Cola" title="Caffeine-Free Coca-Cola">Caffeine-Free Coca-Cola</a></b> (1983–present) – Coca-Cola without the caffeine.</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Coca-Cola_Cherry" title="Coca-Cola Cherry">Coca-Cola Cherry</a></b> (1985–present) – Coca-Cola with a cherry flavor. Was available in Canada starting in 1996. Originally called <b>Cherry Coke (Cherry Coca-Cola)</b> in North America until 2006.</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/New_Coke" title="New Coke">New Coke</a></b> / <b>Coca-Cola II</b> (1985–2002) - An unpopular formula change, remained after the original formula quickly returned and was later rebranded as Coca-Cola II.</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Golden_Coca-Cola" title="Golden Coca-Cola">Golden Coca-Cola</a></b> (2001) was a limited edition produced by Beijing Coca-Cola company to celebrate <a href="/wiki/Bids_for_the_2008_Summer_Olympics" title="Bids for the 2008 Summer Olympics">Beijing's successful bid</a> to host the <a href="/wiki/2008_Summer_Olympics" title="2008 Summer Olympics">Olympics</a>.</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Coca-Cola_with_Lemon" title="Coca-Cola with Lemon">Coca-Cola with Lemon</a></b> (2001–05) – Coca-Cola with a lemon flavor. Available in: Australia, <a href="/wiki/American_Samoa" title="American Samoa">American Samoa</a>, Austria, Belgium, Brazil, China, Denmark, <a href="/wiki/Federation_of_Bosnia_and_Herzegovina" title="Federation of Bosnia and Herzegovina">Federation of Bosnia and Herzegovina</a>, Finland, France, Germany, Hong Kong, Iceland, Korea, Luxembourg, Macau, Malaysia, Mongolia, Netherlands, New Caledonia, New Zealand, <a href="/wiki/R%C3%A9union" title="Réunion">Réunion</a>, Singapore, Spain, Switzerland, Taiwan, Tunisia, United Kingdom, United States and <a href="/wiki/West_Bank" title="West Bank">West Bank</a>-<a href="/wiki/Gaza_Strip" title="Gaza Strip">Gaza</a></li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Coca-Cola_Vanilla" title="Coca-Cola Vanilla">Coca-Cola Vanilla</a></b> (2002–05; 2007–present) – Coca-Cola with a vanilla flavor. Available in: Austria, Australia, China, Czech Republic, Finland, France, Germany, Hong Kong, New Zealand, Malaysia, Slovakia, South-Africa, Sweden, Switzerland, United Kingdom and United States. It was reintroduced in June 2007 by popular demand.</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Coca-Cola_with_Lime" title="Coca-Cola with Lime">Coca-Cola with Lime</a></b> (2005–present) – Coca-Cola with a lime flavor. Available in Belgium, Netherlands, Singapore, Canada, the United Kingdom and the United States.</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Coca-Cola_Raspberry" title="Coca-Cola Raspberry">Coca-Cola Raspberry</a></b> (2005; 2009-present) – Coca-Cola with a raspberry flavor. Originally only available in New Zealand. Available in: Australia, United States and the United Kingdom in <a href="/wiki/Coca-Cola_Freestyle" title="Coca-Cola Freestyle">Coca-Cola Freestyle</a> fountain since 2009.</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Coca-Cola_Black_Cherry_Vanilla" title="Coca-Cola Black Cherry Vanilla">Coca-Cola Black Cherry Vanilla</a></b> (2006–07) – Coca-Cola with a combination of black cherry and vanilla flavor. It replaced and was replaced by Vanilla Coke in June 2007.</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Coca-Cola_Bl%C4%81k" class="mw-redirect" title="Coca-Cola Blāk">Coca-Cola Blāk</a></b> (2006–08) – Coca-Cola with a rich coffee flavor, formula depends on country. Only available in the United States, France, Canada, Czech Republic, Bosnia and Herzegovina, Bulgaria and Lithuania</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Coca-Cola_Citra" title="Coca-Cola Citra">Coca-Cola Citra</a></b> (2005–present) – Coca-Cola with a citrus flavor. Only available in Bosnia and Herzegovina, New Zealand and Japan.</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Coca-Cola_Orange" title="Coca-Cola Orange">Coca-Cola Orange</a></b> (2007) – Coca-Cola with an orange flavor. Was available in the United Kingdom and Gibraltar for a limited time. In Germany, Austria, and Switzerland it is sold under the label <a href="/wiki/Mezzo_Mix" title="Mezzo Mix">Mezzo Mix</a>. Currently available in <a href="/wiki/Coca-Cola_Freestyle" title="Coca-Cola Freestyle">Coca-Cola Freestyle</a> fountain outlets in the United States since 2009 and in the United Kingdom since 2014.</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Coca-Cola_Life" title="Coca-Cola Life">Coca-Cola Life</a></b> (2013–present) – A version of Coca-Cola with <a href="/wiki/Stevia" title="Stevia">stevia</a> and sugar as sweeteners rather than just simply sugar.</li>
<li><b>Coca-Cola Ginger</b> (2016–present) – A version that mixes in the taste of <a href="/wiki/Ginger_beer" title="Ginger beer">ginger beer</a>. Available in Australia, New Zealand and as a limited edition in Vietnam.</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Logo_design"><span id="Logo"></span> Logo design</span></h3>
<p>The Coca-Cola logo was created by John Pemberton's bookkeeper, <a href="/wiki/Frank_Mason_Robinson" title="Frank Mason Robinson">Frank Mason Robinson</a>, in 1885.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> Robinson came up with the name and chose the logo's distinctive cursive script. The writing style used, known as <a href="/wiki/Spencerian_Script" class="mw-redirect" title="Spencerian Script">Spencerian script</a>, was developed in the mid-19th century and was the dominant form of formal handwriting in the United States during that period.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Robinson also played a significant role in early Coca-Cola advertising. His promotional suggestions to Pemberton included giving away thousands of free drink coupons and plastering the city of <a href="/wiki/Atlanta" title="Atlanta">Atlanta</a> with publicity banners and <a href="/wiki/Streetcar" class="mw-redirect" title="Streetcar">streetcar</a> signs.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Contour_bottle_design">Contour bottle design</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">"Coke bottle" redirects here. For the song, see <a href="/wiki/Coke_Bottle_(song)" title="Coke Bottle (song)">Coke Bottle (song)</a>.</div>
<p>The  Coca-Cola bottle, called the "contour bottle" within the company, was created by bottle designer <a href="/wiki/Earl_R._Dean" title="Earl R. Dean">Earl R. Dean</a> and Coca-Cola's <a href="/wiki/General_counsel" title="General counsel">general counsel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Harold_Hirsch" title="Harold Hirsch">Harold Hirsch</a>. In 1915, The Coca-Cola Company was represented by their general counsel to launch a competition among its bottle suppliers as well as any competition entrants to create a new bottle for their beverage that would distinguish it from other beverage bottles, "a bottle which a person could recognize even if they felt it in the dark, and so shaped that, even if broken, a person could tell at a glance what it was."<sup id="cite_ref-vigo.lib.in.us_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vigo.lib.in.us-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-His_Life_and_Madness_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-His_Life_and_Madness-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Chapman J. Root, president of <a href="/wiki/The_Root_Glass_Company" title="The Root Glass Company">the Root Glass Company</a> of <a href="/wiki/Terre_Haute,_Indiana" title="Terre Haute, Indiana">Terre Haute, Indiana</a>, turned the project over to members of his supervisory staff, including company auditor T. Clyde Edwards, plant superintendent Alexander Samuelsson, and <a href="/wiki/Earl_R._Dean" title="Earl R. Dean">Earl R. Dean</a>, bottle designer and supervisor of the bottle molding room. Root and his subordinates decided to base the bottle's design on one of the soda's two ingredients, the <a href="/wiki/Coca_leaf" class="mw-redirect" title="Coca leaf">coca leaf</a> or the <a href="/wiki/Kola_nut" title="Kola nut">kola nut</a>, but were unaware of what either ingredient looked like. Dean and Edwards went to the <a href="/wiki/Emeline_Fairbanks_Memorial_Library" title="Emeline Fairbanks Memorial Library">Emeline Fairbanks Memorial Library</a> and were unable to find any information about coca or kola. Instead, Dean was inspired by a picture of the gourd-shaped <a href="/wiki/Cocoa_pod" class="mw-redirect" title="Cocoa pod">cocoa pod</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Encyclop%C3%A6dia_Britannica" title="Encyclopædia Britannica">Encyclopædia Britannica</a>. Dean made a rough sketch of the pod and returned to the plant to show Root. He explained to Root how he could transform the shape of the pod into a bottle. Root gave Dean his approval.<sup id="cite_ref-vigo.lib.in.us_83-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vigo.lib.in.us-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Faced with the upcoming scheduled maintenance of the mold-making machinery, over the next 24 hours Dean sketched out a concept drawing which was approved by Root the next morning. Dean then proceeded to create a bottle mold and produced a small number of bottles before the glass-molding machinery was turned off.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Chapman Root approved the prototype bottle and a <a href="/wiki/Design_patent" title="Design patent">design patent</a> was issued on the bottle in November 1915. The prototype never made it to production since its middle diameter was larger than its base, making it unstable on <a href="/wiki/Conveyor_belt" title="Conveyor belt">conveyor belts</a>. Dean resolved this issue by decreasing the bottle's middle diameter. During the 1916 bottler's convention, Dean's contour bottle was chosen over other entries and was on the market the same year. By 1920, the contour bottle became the standard for The Coca-Cola Company. A revised version was also patented in 1923. Because the Patent Office releases the <i>Patent Gazette</i> on Tuesday, the bottle was patented on December 25, 1923, and was nicknamed the "Christmas bottle." Today, the contour Coca-Cola bottle is one of the most recognized packages on the planet..."even in the dark!".<sup id="cite_ref-history-of-bottling_32-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-history-of-bottling-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>As a reward for his efforts, Dean was offered a choice between a $500 bonus or a lifetime job at <a href="/wiki/The_Root_Glass_Company" title="The Root Glass Company">the Root Glass Company</a>. He chose the lifetime job and kept it until the <a href="/wiki/Owens-Illinois_Glass_Company" class="mw-redirect" title="Owens-Illinois Glass Company">Owens-Illinois Glass Company</a> bought out <a href="/wiki/The_Root_Glass_Company" title="The Root Glass Company">the Root Glass Company</a> in the mid-1930s. Dean went on to work in other Midwestern glass factories.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>One alternative depiction has <a href="/wiki/Raymond_Loewy" title="Raymond Loewy">Raymond Loewy</a> as the inventor of the unique design, but, while Loewy did serve as a designer of Coke cans and bottles in later years, he was in the <a href="/wiki/French_Army" title="French Army">French Army</a> the year the bottle was invented and did not emigrate to the United States until 1919. Others have attributed inspiration for the design not to the cocoa pod, but to a <a href="/wiki/Victorian_fashion" title="Victorian fashion">Victorian</a> <a href="/wiki/Hoop_skirt" title="Hoop skirt">hooped dress</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1944, Associate Justice <a href="/wiki/Roger_J._Traynor" title="Roger J. Traynor">Roger J. Traynor</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_California" title="Supreme Court of California">Supreme Court of California</a> took advantage of a case involving a waitress injured by an exploding Coca-Cola bottle to articulate the doctrine of <a href="/wiki/Strict_liability" title="Strict liability">strict liability</a> for <a href="/wiki/Product_liability" title="Product liability">defective products</a>. Traynor's <a href="/wiki/Concurring_opinion" title="Concurring opinion">concurring opinion</a> in <i><a href="/wiki/Escola_v._Coca-Cola_Bottling_Co." title="Escola v. Coca-Cola Bottling Co.">Escola v. Coca-Cola Bottling Co.</a></i> is widely recognized as a landmark case in U.S. law today.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Types">Types</span></h3>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Designer_bottles">Designer bottles</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Karl_Lagerfeld" title="Karl Lagerfeld">Karl Lagerfeld</a> is the latest designer to have created a collection of aluminum bottles for Coca-Cola. Lagerfeld is not the first fashion designer to create a special version of the famous Coca-Cola Contour bottle. A number of other limited edition bottles by fashion designers for Coca-Cola Light soda have been created in the last few years.
</p><p>In 2009, in Italy, Coca-Cola Light had a Tribute to Fashion to celebrate 100 years of the recognizable contour bottle. Well known Italian designers Alberta Ferretti, Blumarine, Etro, Fendi, Marni, Missoni, Moschino, and Versace each designed limited edition bottles.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Competitors">Competitors</span></h2>
<p><a href="/wiki/Pepsi" title="Pepsi">Pepsi</a>, the flagship product of <a href="/wiki/PepsiCo" title="PepsiCo">PepsiCo</a>, The Coca-Cola Company's main rival in the soft drink industry, is usually second to Coke in sales, and outsells Coca-Cola in some markets. <a href="/wiki/RC_Cola" title="RC Cola">RC Cola</a>, now owned by the <a href="/wiki/Dr_Pepper_Snapple_Group" title="Dr Pepper Snapple Group">Dr Pepper Snapple Group</a>, the third largest soft drink manufacturer, is also widely available.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>Around the world, many local brands compete with Coke. In South and Central America <a href="/wiki/Kola_Real" title="Kola Real">Kola Real</a>, known as <a href="/wiki/Big_Cola" class="mw-redirect" title="Big Cola">Big Cola</a> in Mexico, is a growing competitor to Coca-Cola.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> On the French island of <a href="/wiki/Corsica" title="Corsica">Corsica</a>, <a href="/wiki/Corsica_Cola" title="Corsica Cola">Corsica Cola</a>, made by brewers of the local <a href="/wiki/Pietra_Brewery" title="Pietra Brewery">Pietra beer</a>, is a growing competitor to Coca-Cola. In the French region of <a href="/wiki/Brittany" title="Brittany">Brittany</a>, <a href="/wiki/Breizh_Cola" title="Breizh Cola">Breizh Cola</a> is available. In Peru, <a href="/wiki/Inca_Kola" title="Inca Kola">Inca Kola</a> outsells Coca-Cola, which led The Coca-Cola Company to purchase the brand in 1999. In Sweden, <a href="/wiki/Julmust" title="Julmust">Julmust</a> outsells Coca-Cola during the Christmas season.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> In Scotland, the locally produced <a href="/wiki/Irn-Bru" title="Irn-Bru">Irn-Bru</a> was more popular than Coca-Cola until 2005, when Coca-Cola and Diet Coke began to outpace its sales.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> In the former <a href="/wiki/East_Germany" title="East Germany">East Germany</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vita_Cola" title="Vita Cola">Vita Cola</a>, invented during Communist rule, is gaining popularity.
</p><p>In India, Coca-Cola ranked third behind the leader, Pepsi-Cola, and local drink <a href="/wiki/Thums_Up" title="Thums Up">Thums Up</a>. The Coca-Cola Company purchased Thums Up in 1993.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> As of  2004<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Coca-Cola&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>, Coca-Cola held a 60.9% market-share in India.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> Tropicola, a domestic drink, is served in Cuba instead of Coca-Cola, due to a United States embargo. French brand <a href="/wiki/Mecca_Cola" class="mw-redirect" title="Mecca Cola">Mecca Cola</a> and British brand <a href="/wiki/Qibla_Cola" title="Qibla Cola">Qibla Cola</a> are competitors to Coca-Cola in the Middle East.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>In Turkey, <a href="/wiki/Cola_Turka" title="Cola Turka">Cola Turka</a>, in Iran and the Middle East, <a href="/wiki/Zamzam_Cola" class="mw-redirect" title="Zamzam Cola">Zamzam Cola</a> and <a href="/wiki/Parsi_Cola" title="Parsi Cola">Parsi Cola</a>, in some parts of China, <a href="/wiki/China_Cola" class="mw-redirect" title="China Cola">China Cola</a>, in Slovenia, <a href="/wiki/Cockta" title="Cockta">Cockta</a>, and the inexpensive Mercator Cola, sold only in the country's biggest supermarket chain, <a href="/wiki/Mercator_(retail)" title="Mercator (retail)">Mercator</a>, are some of the brand's competitors. Classiko Cola, made by Tiko Group, the largest manufacturing company in Madagascar, is a competitor to Coca-Cola in many regions.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Advertising">Advertising</span></h2>
