<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Cocacola-5cents-1900_edit2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Cocacola-5cents-1900_edit2.jpg/170px-Cocacola-5cents-1900_edit2.jpg" width="170" height="233" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Cocacola-5cents-1900_edit2.jpg/255px-Cocacola-5cents-1900_edit2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Cocacola-5cents-1900_edit2.jpg/340px-Cocacola-5cents-1900_edit2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2331" data-file-height="3190" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Cocacola-5cents-1900_edit2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An 1890s advertisement showing model <a href="/wiki/Hilda_Clark_(model)" title="Hilda Clark (model)">Hilda Clark</a> in formal <a href="/wiki/Victorian_fashion" title="Victorian fashion">19th-century attire</a>. The ad is titled <a href="/wiki/The_fixed_price_of_Coca-Cola_from_1886_to_1959" class="mw-redirect" title="The fixed price of Coca-Cola from 1886 to 1959"><i>Drink Coca-Cola 5¢</i></a>. (US).</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Troop_B,_1st_Cavalry_Regiment,_Washington_National_Guard.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/ae/Troop_B%2C_1st_Cavalry_Regiment%2C_Washington_National_Guard.jpg/220px-Troop_B%2C_1st_Cavalry_Regiment%2C_Washington_National_Guard.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/ae/Troop_B%2C_1st_Cavalry_Regiment%2C_Washington_National_Guard.jpg/330px-Troop_B%2C_1st_Cavalry_Regiment%2C_Washington_National_Guard.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/ae/Troop_B%2C_1st_Cavalry_Regiment%2C_Washington_National_Guard.jpg/440px-Troop_B%2C_1st_Cavalry_Regiment%2C_Washington_National_Guard.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3008" data-file-height="2005" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Troop_B,_1st_Cavalry_Regiment,_Washington_National_Guard.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A Coca-Cola billboard seen in <a href="/wiki/Tacoma,_Washington" title="Tacoma, Washington">Tacoma, Washington</a> in 1907 during a parade by <a href="/wiki/Troop_B,_Washington_Cavalry" title="Troop B, Washington Cavalry">Troop B, Washington Cavalry</a>.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ft_Dodge_ghost_sign.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Ft_Dodge_ghost_sign.jpg/220px-Ft_Dodge_ghost_sign.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Ft_Dodge_ghost_sign.jpg/330px-Ft_Dodge_ghost_sign.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Ft_Dodge_ghost_sign.jpg/440px-Ft_Dodge_ghost_sign.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2592" data-file-height="1944" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ft_Dodge_ghost_sign.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Coca-Cola <a href="/wiki/Ghost_sign" title="Ghost sign">ghost sign</a> in <a href="/wiki/Fort_Dodge,_Iowa" title="Fort Dodge, Iowa">Fort Dodge, Iowa</a>. Older Coca-Cola ghosts behind Borax and telephone ads. April 2008.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Camion_cocacola_argentina_1942.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Camion_cocacola_argentina_1942.jpg/220px-Camion_cocacola_argentina_1942.jpg" width="220" height="167" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Camion_cocacola_argentina_1942.jpg/330px-Camion_cocacola_argentina_1942.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Camion_cocacola_argentina_1942.jpg/440px-Camion_cocacola_argentina_1942.jpg 2x" data-file-width="960" data-file-height="728" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Camion_cocacola_argentina_1942.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Coca-Cola delivery truck of Argentina, with the slogan "Drink Coca-Cola – delicious, refreshing".</div></div></div>
<p>Coca-Cola's advertising has significantly affected <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_the_United_States" title="Culture of the United States">American culture</a>, and it is frequently credited with inventing the modern image of <a href="/wiki/Santa_Claus" title="Santa Claus">Santa Claus</a> as an old man in a red-and-white suit. Although the company did start using the red-and-white Santa image in the 1930s, with its winter advertising campaigns illustrated by <a href="/wiki/Haddon_Sundblom" title="Haddon Sundblom">Haddon Sundblom</a>, the motif was already common.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-usir.salford.ac.uk_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-usir.salford.ac.uk-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> Coca-Cola was not even the first soft drink company to use the modern image of Santa Claus in its advertising: <a href="/wiki/White_Rock_Beverages" title="White Rock Beverages">White Rock Beverages</a> used Santa in advertisements for its <a href="/wiki/Ginger_ale" title="Ginger ale">ginger ale</a> in 1923, after first using him to sell <a href="/wiki/Mineral_water" title="Mineral water">mineral water</a> in 1915.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> Before Santa Claus, Coca-Cola relied on images of smartly dressed young women to sell its beverages. Coca-Cola's first such advertisement appeared in 1895, featuring the young Bostonian actress <a href="/wiki/Hilda_Clark_(model)" title="Hilda Clark (model)">Hilda Clark</a> as its spokeswoman.
</p><p>1941 saw the first use of the nickname "Coke" as an official trademark for the product, with a series of advertisements informing consumers that "Coke means Coca-Cola".<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> In 1971, a song from a Coca-Cola commercial called "<a href="/wiki/I%27d_Like_to_Teach_the_World_to_Sing" class="mw-redirect" title="I&#39;d Like to Teach the World to Sing">I'd Like to Teach the World to Sing</a>", produced by <a href="/wiki/Roquel_Billy_Davis" class="mw-redirect" title="Roquel Billy Davis">Billy Davis</a>, became a <a href="/wiki/Hit_single" title="Hit single">hit single</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sample_You_2_typeface.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Sample_You_2_typeface.png/220px-Sample_You_2_typeface.png" width="220" height="286" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Sample_You_2_typeface.png/330px-Sample_You_2_typeface.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Sample_You_2_typeface.png/440px-Sample_You_2_typeface.png 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="1040" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sample_You_2_typeface.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The typeface <b>You 2</b> that was created for the "Share a Coke" campaign</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Cabo_Verde_Fogo_CokeBooth.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Cabo_Verde_Fogo_CokeBooth.JPG/220px-Cabo_Verde_Fogo_CokeBooth.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Cabo_Verde_Fogo_CokeBooth.JPG/330px-Cabo_Verde_Fogo_CokeBooth.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Cabo_Verde_Fogo_CokeBooth.JPG/440px-Cabo_Verde_Fogo_CokeBooth.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1200" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Cabo_Verde_Fogo_CokeBooth.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Coca-Cola sales booth on the Cape Verde island of <a href="/wiki/Fogo,_Cape_Verde" title="Fogo, Cape Verde">Fogo</a> in 2004.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Coca-cola_(2).JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Coca-cola_%282%29.JPG/170px-Coca-cola_%282%29.JPG" width="170" height="227" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Coca-cola_%282%29.JPG/255px-Coca-cola_%282%29.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Coca-cola_%282%29.JPG/340px-Coca-cola_%282%29.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3240" data-file-height="4320" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Coca-cola_(2).JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Coke advertisement in Budapest, 2013.</div></div></div>
<p>Coke's advertising is pervasive, as one of <a href="/wiki/Ernest_Woodruff" title="Ernest Woodruff">Woodruff</a>'s stated goals was to ensure that everyone on Earth drank Coca-Cola as their preferred beverage. This is especially true in southern areas of the United States, such as <a href="/wiki/Atlanta" title="Atlanta">Atlanta</a>, where Coke was born.
</p><p>Some Coca-Cola <a href="/wiki/Television_advertisement" title="Television advertisement">television commercials</a> between 1960 through 1986 were written and produced by former Atlanta radio veteran <a href="/wiki/Don_Naylor" title="Don Naylor">Don Naylor</a> (<a href="/wiki/WGST" title="WGST">WGST</a> 1936–1950, <a href="/wiki/WAGA_(TV)" class="mw-redirect" title="WAGA (TV)">WAGA</a> 1951–1959) during his career as a producer for the <a href="/wiki/McCann_Erickson" class="mw-redirect" title="McCann Erickson">McCann Erickson</a> <a href="/wiki/Advertising_agency" title="Advertising agency">advertising agency</a>. Many of these early television commercials for Coca-Cola featured movie stars, sports heroes, and popular singers.
</p><p>During the 1980s, <a href="/wiki/Pepsi-Cola" class="mw-redirect" title="Pepsi-Cola">Pepsi-Cola</a> ran a series of television advertisements showing people participating in taste tests demonstrating that, according to the commercials, "fifty percent of the participants who said they preferred Coke <i>actually</i> chose the Pepsi." Statisticians pointed out the problematic nature of a 50/50 result: most likely, the taste tests showed that in blind tests, most people cannot tell the difference between Pepsi and Coke. Coca-Cola ran ads to combat Pepsi's ads in an incident sometimes referred to as the <i><a href="/wiki/Cola_wars" title="Cola wars">cola wars</a></i>; one of Coke's ads compared the so-called <a href="/wiki/Pepsi_challenge" class="mw-redirect" title="Pepsi challenge">Pepsi challenge</a> to two <a href="/wiki/Chimpanzee" title="Chimpanzee">chimpanzees</a> deciding which <a href="/wiki/Tennis_ball" title="Tennis ball">tennis ball</a> was furrier. Thereafter, Coca-Cola regained its leadership in the market.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Selena" title="Selena">Selena</a> was a spokesperson for Coca-Cola from 1989 until the time of her death. She filmed three commercials for the company. During 1994, to commemorate her five years with the company, Coca-Cola issued special Selena coke bottles.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Coca-Cola Company purchased <a href="/wiki/Columbia_Pictures" title="Columbia Pictures">Columbia Pictures</a> in 1982, and began inserting Coke-product images into many of its films. After a few early successes during Coca-Cola's ownership, Columbia began to under-perform, and the studio was sold to <a href="/wiki/Sony" title="Sony">Sony</a> in 1989.
</p><p>Coca-Cola has gone through a number of different <a href="/wiki/Advertising_slogan" title="Advertising slogan">advertising slogans</a> in its long history, including "The pause that refreshes", "I'd like to buy the world a Coke", and "Coke is it".
</p><p>In 2006, Coca-Cola introduced <a href="/wiki/My_Coke_Rewards" title="My Coke Rewards">My Coke Rewards</a>, a customer loyalty campaign where consumers earn points by entering codes from specially marked packages of Coca-Cola products into a website. These points can be redeemed for various prizes or sweepstakes entries.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In Australia in 2011, Coca-Cola began the "share a Coke" campaign, where the Coca-Cola logo was replaced on the bottles and replaced with first names. Coca-Cola used the 150 most popular names in Australia to print on the bottles.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> The campaign was paired with a website page, Facebook page, and an online "share a virtual Coke". The same campaign was introduced to Coca-Cola, Diet Coke &amp; Coke Zero bottles and cans in the UK in 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Coca-Cola has also advertised its product to be consumed as a breakfast beverage, instead of coffee or tea for the morning caffeine.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="5_cents">5 cents</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/The_fixed_price_of_Coca-Cola_from_1886_to_1959" class="mw-redirect" title="The fixed price of Coca-Cola from 1886 to 1959">The fixed price of Coca-Cola from 1886 to 1959</a></div>
<p>From 1886 to 1959, the price of Coca-Cola was fixed at five cents, in part due to an advertising campaign.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Holiday_campaigns">Holiday campaigns</span></h3>
<p>Throughout the years, Coca-Cola has released limited time collector bottles for Christmas.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Weihnachtstruck.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Weihnachtstruck.jpg/170px-Weihnachtstruck.jpg" width="170" height="227" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Weihnachtstruck.jpg/255px-Weihnachtstruck.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Weihnachtstruck.jpg/340px-Weihnachtstruck.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1488" data-file-height="1984" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Weihnachtstruck.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Freightliner_Trucks" title="Freightliner Trucks">Freightliner</a> Coca-Cola Christmas truck in <a href="/wiki/Dresden" title="Dresden">Dresden</a>, Germany, 2004.</div></div></div>
<p>The "Holidays are coming!" advertisement features a train of red delivery trucks, emblazoned with the Coca-Cola name and decorated with <a href="/wiki/Christmas_lights" title="Christmas lights">Christmas lights</a>, driving through a snowy landscape and causing everything that they pass to light up and people to watch as they pass through.<sup id="cite_ref-Sandison_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sandison-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The advertisement fell into disuse in 2001, as the Coca-Cola company restructured its advertising campaigns so that advertising around the world was produced locally in each country, rather than centrally in the company's headquarters in Atlanta, Georgia.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> In 2007, the company brought back the campaign after, according to the company, many consumers telephoned its information center saying that they considered it to mark the beginning of Christmas.<sup id="cite_ref-Sandison_110-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sandison-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> The advertisement was created by U.S. advertising agency Doner, and has been part of the company's global advertising campaign for many years.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Keith Law, a producer and writer of commercials for <a href="/wiki/Belfast_CityBeat" title="Belfast CityBeat">Belfast CityBeat</a>, was not convinced by Coca-Cola's reintroduction of the advertisement in 2007, saying that "I don't think there's anything Christmassy about <a href="/wiki/Heavy_Goods_Vehicle" class="mw-redirect" title="Heavy Goods Vehicle">HGVs</a> and the commercial is too generic."<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2001, singer <a href="/wiki/Melanie_Thornton" title="Melanie Thornton">Melanie Thornton</a> recorded the campaign's advertising jingle as a single, <i>Wonderful Dream (Holidays are Coming)</i>, which entered the pop-music charts in Germany at no. 9.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> In 2005, Coca-Cola expanded the advertising campaign to radio, employing several variations of the jingle.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2011, Coca-Cola launched a campaign for the Indian holiday <a href="/wiki/Diwali" title="Diwali">Diwali</a>. The campaign included commercials, a song, and an integration with Shah Rukh Khan's film <i><a href="/wiki/Ra.One" title="Ra.One">Ra.One</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sports_sponsorship">Sports sponsorship</span></h3>
<p>Coca-Cola was the first commercial <a href="/wiki/Sponsor_(commercial)" title="Sponsor (commercial)">sponsor</a> of the Olympic games, at the <a href="/wiki/1928_Summer_Olympics" title="1928 Summer Olympics">1928 games</a> in Amsterdam, and has been an Olympics sponsor ever since.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> This corporate sponsorship included the <a href="/wiki/1996_Summer_Olympics" title="1996 Summer Olympics">1996 Summer Olympics</a> hosted in <a href="/wiki/Atlanta" title="Atlanta">Atlanta</a>, which allowed Coca-Cola to spotlight its hometown. Most recently, Coca-Cola has released localized commercials for the <a href="/wiki/2010_Winter_Olympics" title="2010 Winter Olympics">2010 Winter Olympics</a> in Vancouver; one Canadian commercial referred to Canada's hockey heritage and was modified after Canada won the gold medal game on February 28, 2010 by changing the ending line of the commercial to say "Now they know whose game they're playing".<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Since <a href="/wiki/1978_FIFA_World_Cup" title="1978 FIFA World Cup">1978</a>, Coca-Cola has sponsored the <a href="/wiki/FIFA_World_Cup" title="FIFA World Cup">FIFA World Cup</a>, and other competitions organized by FIFA.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> One <a href="/wiki/FIFA" title="FIFA">FIFA</a> tournament trophy, the <a href="/wiki/FIFA_U-20_World_Cup" title="FIFA U-20 World Cup">FIFA World Youth Championship</a> from Tunisia in <a href="/wiki/1977_FIFA_World_Youth_Championship" title="1977 FIFA World Youth Championship">1977</a> to Malaysia in <a href="/wiki/1997_FIFA_World_Youth_Championship" title="1997 FIFA World Youth Championship">1997</a>, was called "FIFA&#160;— Coca-Cola Cup". In addition, Coca-Cola sponsors the annual <a href="/wiki/Coca-Cola_600" title="Coca-Cola 600">Coca-Cola 600</a> and <a href="/wiki/Coke_Zero_400" class="mw-redirect" title="Coke Zero 400">Coke Zero 400</a> for the <a href="/wiki/NASCAR" title="NASCAR">NASCAR</a> <a href="/wiki/Sprint_Cup_Series" class="mw-redirect" title="Sprint Cup Series">Sprint Cup Series</a> at <a href="/wiki/Charlotte_Motor_Speedway" title="Charlotte Motor Speedway">Charlotte Motor Speedway</a> in <a href="/wiki/Concord,_North_Carolina" title="Concord, North Carolina">Concord, North Carolina</a> and <a href="/wiki/Daytona_International_Speedway" title="Daytona International Speedway">Daytona International Speedway</a> in Daytona, Florida.
</p><p>Coca-Cola has a long history of sports marketing relationships, which over the years have included <a href="/wiki/Major_League_Baseball" title="Major League Baseball">Major League Baseball</a>, the <a href="/wiki/National_Football_League" title="National Football League">National Football League</a>, the <a href="/wiki/National_Basketball_Association" title="National Basketball Association">National Basketball Association</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/National_Hockey_League" title="National Hockey League">National Hockey League</a>, as well as with many teams within those leagues. Coca-Cola has had a longtime relationship with the NFL's <a href="/wiki/Pittsburgh_Steelers" title="Pittsburgh Steelers">Pittsburgh Steelers</a>, due in part to the now-famous <a href="/wiki/Hey_Kid,_Catch!" title="Hey Kid, Catch!">1979 television commercial</a> featuring <a href="/wiki/Joe_Greene_(American_football)" class="mw-redirect" title="Joe Greene (American football)">"Mean Joe" Greene</a>, leading to the two opening the Coca-Cola Great Hall at <a href="/wiki/Heinz_Field" title="Heinz Field">Heinz Field</a> in 2001 and a more recent <a href="/wiki/Coca-Cola_Zero" class="mw-redirect" title="Coca-Cola Zero">Coca-Cola Zero</a> commercial featuring <a href="/wiki/Troy_Polamalu" title="Troy Polamalu">Troy Polamalu</a>.
</p><p>Coca-Cola is the official soft drink of many <a href="/wiki/College_football" title="College football">collegiate football</a> teams throughout the nation, partly due to Coca-Cola providing those schools with upgraded athletic facilities in exchange for Coca-Cola's sponsorship. This is especially prevalent at the high school level, which is more dependent on such contracts due to tighter budgets.
</p><p>Coca-Cola was one of the official sponsors of the <a href="/wiki/1996_Cricket_World_Cup" title="1996 Cricket World Cup">1996 Cricket World Cup</a> held on the <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">Indian subcontinent</a>. Coca-Cola is also one of the associate sponsors of <a href="/wiki/Delhi_Daredevils" title="Delhi Daredevils">Delhi Daredevils</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Premier_League" title="Indian Premier League">Indian Premier League</a>.
</p><p>In England, Coca-Cola was the main sponsor of <a href="/wiki/The_Football_League" class="mw-redirect" title="The Football League">The Football League</a> between 2004 and 2010, a name given to the three professional divisions below the <a href="/wiki/Premier_League" title="Premier League">Premier League</a> in <a href="/wiki/Association_football" title="Association football">soccer</a> (football). In 2005, Coca-Cola launched a competition for the 72 clubs of The Football League&#160;— it was called "Win a Player". This allowed fans to place one vote per day for their favorite club, with one entry being chosen at random earning £250,000 for the club; this was repeated in 2006. The "Win A Player" competition was very controversial, as at the end of the 2 competitions, <a href="/wiki/Leeds_United_A.F.C." class="mw-redirect" title="Leeds United A.F.C.">Leeds United A.F.C.</a> had the most votes by more than double, yet they did not win any money to spend on a new player for the club. In 2007, the competition changed to "Buy a Player". This competition allowed fans to buy a bottle of Coca-Cola or Coca-Cola Zero and submit the code on the wrapper on the Coca-Cola website. This code could then earn anything from 50p to £100,000 for a club of their choice. This competition was favored over the old "Win a Player" competition, as it allowed all clubs to win some money.  Between 1992 and 1998, Coca-Cola was the title sponsor of the <a href="/wiki/Football_League_Cup" class="mw-redirect" title="Football League Cup">Football League Cup</a> (Coca-Cola Cup), the secondary cup tournament of England.
</p><p>Between 1994 and 1997, Coca-Cola was also the title sponsor of the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_League_Cup" title="Scottish League Cup">Scottish League Cup</a>, renaming it the Coca-Cola Cup like its English counterpart. From 1998 to 2001, the company were the title sponsor of the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland_Football_League_Cup" title="Northern Ireland Football League Cup">Irish League Cup</a> in <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland" title="Northern Ireland">Northern Ireland</a>, where it was named the Coca-Cola League Cup.
</p><p>Coca-Cola is the presenting sponsor of the <a href="/wiki/Tour_Championship" title="Tour Championship">Tour Championship</a>, the final event of the <a href="/wiki/PGA_Tour" title="PGA Tour">PGA Tour</a> held each year at <a href="/wiki/East_Lake_Golf_Club" title="East Lake Golf Club">East Lake Golf Club</a> in <a href="/wiki/Atlanta" title="Atlanta">Atlanta</a>, GA.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Introduced March 1, 2010, in Canada, to celebrate the <a href="/wiki/2010_Winter_Olympics" title="2010 Winter Olympics">2010 Winter Olympics</a>, Coca-Cola sold gold colored cans in packs of 12 355&#160;mL (12&#160;imp&#160;fl&#160;oz; 12&#160;US&#160;fl&#160;oz) each, in select stores.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_mass_media">In mass media</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Jersey_Gardens_Mall_11.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Jersey_Gardens_Mall_11.jpg/220px-Jersey_Gardens_Mall_11.jpg" width="220" height="293" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Jersey_Gardens_Mall_11.jpg/330px-Jersey_Gardens_Mall_11.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Jersey_Gardens_Mall_11.jpg/440px-Jersey_Gardens_Mall_11.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3864" data-file-height="5152" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Jersey_Gardens_Mall_11.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Coca-Cola vending machine.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:VWT2_Coca_Cola.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/VWT2_Coca_Cola.JPG/220px-VWT2_Coca_Cola.JPG" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/VWT2_Coca_Cola.JPG/330px-VWT2_Coca_Cola.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/VWT2_Coca_Cola.JPG/440px-VWT2_Coca_Cola.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2513" data-file-height="1673" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:VWT2_Coca_Cola.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Coca-Cola advertised on a <a href="/wiki/Volkswagen_T2" class="mw-redirect" title="Volkswagen T2">Volkswagen T2</a> in <a href="/wiki/Maring%C3%A1" title="Maringá">Maringá</a>, <a href="/wiki/Paran%C3%A1_(state)" title="Paraná (state)">Paraná</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a>, 2012.</div></div></div>
<p>Coca-Cola has been prominently featured in many films and television programs. It was a major plot element in films such as <a href="/wiki/One,_Two,_Three" title="One, Two, Three">One, Two, Three</a>, <a href="/wiki/The_Coca-Cola_Kid" title="The Coca-Cola Kid">The Coca-Cola Kid</a>, and <a href="/wiki/The_Gods_Must_Be_Crazy" title="The Gods Must Be Crazy">The Gods Must Be Crazy</a>, among many others. In music, in <a href="/wiki/The_Beatles" title="The Beatles">the Beatles</a>' song, "<a href="/wiki/Come_Together" title="Come Together">Come Together</a>", the lyrics say, "He shoot Coca-Cola", he say.... <a href="/wiki/The_Beach_Boys" title="The Beach Boys">The Beach Boys</a> also referenced Coca-Cola in their 1964 song "All Summer Long" (i.e. Member when you spilled Coke all over your blouse?)<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The best selling artist of all time<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Elvis_Presley" title="Elvis Presley">Elvis Presley</a>, promoted Coca-Cola during his <a href="/wiki/Elvis_in_Concert" title="Elvis in Concert">last tour of 1977</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> The Coca-Cola Company used Elvis' image to promote the product.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> For example, the company used a song performed by Presley, <a href="/wiki/A_Little_Less_Conversation" title="A Little Less Conversation">A Little Less Conversation</a>, in a Japanese Coca-Cola commercial.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Other artists that promoted Coca-Cola include <a href="/wiki/David_Bowie" title="David Bowie">David Bowie</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/George_Michael" title="George Michael">George Michael</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Elton_John" title="Elton John">Elton John</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Whitney_Houston" title="Whitney Houston">Whitney Houston</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> who appeared in the Diet Coca-Cola commercial, among many others.
</p><p>Not all musical references to Coca-Cola went well.  A line in "<a href="/wiki/Lola_(song)" title="Lola (song)">Lola</a>" by <a href="/wiki/The_Kinks" title="The Kinks">the Kinks</a> was originally recorded as "You drink champagne and it tastes just like Coca-Cola."  When the <a href="/wiki/British_Broadcasting_Corporation" class="mw-redirect" title="British Broadcasting Corporation">British Broadcasting Corporation</a> refused to play the song because of the commercial reference, lead singer <a href="/wiki/Ray_Davies" title="Ray Davies">Ray Davies</a> re-recorded the lyric as "it tastes just like cherry cola" to get airplay for the song.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Political cartoonist <a href="/wiki/Michel_Kichka" title="Michel Kichka">Michel Kichka</a> satirized a famous Coca-Cola billboard in his 1982 poster "And I Love New York."  On the billboard, the Coca-Cola wave is accompanied by the words "Enjoy Coke." In <a href="/w/index.php?title=Kichka&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Kichka (page does not exist)">Kichka</a>'s poster, the lettering and script above the Coca-Cola wave instead read "Enjoy Cocaine."<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Medicinal_application">Medicinal application</span></h2>
<p>Coca-Cola is sometimes used for the <a href="/wiki/Coca-Cola_treatment_of_phytobezoars" class="mw-redirect" title="Coca-Cola treatment of phytobezoars">treatment</a> of gastric <a href="/wiki/Phytobezoar" title="Phytobezoar">phytobezoars</a>. In about 50% of cases studied, Coca-Cola alone was found to be effective in gastric phytobezoar dissolution. Unfortunately, this treatment can result in the potential of developing small bowel obstruction in a minority of cases, necessitating surgical intervention.<sup id="cite_ref-Iwamuro2015_136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Iwamuro2015-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Criticism">Criticism</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Criticism_of_Coca-Cola" title="Criticism of Coca-Cola">Criticism of Coca-Cola</a></div>
<p>Criticism of Coca-Cola has arisen from various groups, concerning a variety of issues, including <a href="/wiki/Health_effects" class="mw-redirect" title="Health effects">health effects</a>, <a href="/wiki/Environmental_issues" class="mw-redirect" title="Environmental issues">environmental issues</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Business_practice" class="mw-redirect" title="Business practice">business practices</a>. The drink's coca flavoring, and the nickname "Coke", remain a common  theme of criticism due to the relationship with the illegal drug <a href="/wiki/Cocaine" title="Cocaine">cocaine</a>. In 1911, the US government seized 40 barrels and 20 kegs of Coca-Cola syrup in <a href="/wiki/Chattanooga,_Tennessee" title="Chattanooga, Tennessee">Chattanooga, Tennessee</a>, alleging the caffeine in its drink was "injurious to health", leading to amended food safety legislation.<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Coca-Cola Company, its subsidiaries and products have been subject to sustained criticism by both <a href="/wiki/Consumer_group" class="mw-redirect" title="Consumer group">consumer groups</a>, <a href="/wiki/Left-wing_politics" title="Left-wing politics">leftist activists</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Consumer_watchdog" title="Consumer watchdog">watchdogs</a>, particularly since the early 2000s.<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Coca-Cola is rich in sugar, especially <a href="/wiki/Sucrose" title="Sucrose">sucrose</a>, which causes <a href="/wiki/Dental_caries" class="mw-redirect" title="Dental caries">dental caries</a> when consumed regularly. Besides this, the high caloric value contributes to <a href="/wiki/Obesity" title="Obesity">obesity</a>. Both are major health issues in the developed world.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Colombian_death-squad_allegations">Colombian death-squad allegations</span></h2>
<p>In July 2001, the Coca-Cola company was sued over its alleged use of political <a href="/wiki/Far-right" class="mw-redirect" title="Far-right">far-right</a> wing <a href="/wiki/Death_squads" class="mw-redirect" title="Death squads">death squads</a> (the <a href="/wiki/United_Self-Defense_Forces_of_Colombia" title="United Self-Defense Forces of Colombia">United Self-Defense Forces of Colombia</a>) to kidnap, torture, and kill Colombian bottler workers that were linked with trade union activity. Coca-Cola was sued in a US federal court in Miami by the Colombian food and drink union Sinaltrainal. The suit alleged that Coca-Cola was indirectly responsible for having "contracted with or otherwise directed <a href="/wiki/Paramilitary" title="Paramilitary">paramilitary</a> security forces that utilized extreme violence and murdered, tortured, unlawfully detained or otherwise silenced trade union leaders". This sparked campaigns to boycott Coca-Cola in the UK, US, Germany, Italy, and Australia.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Guardian_142-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Guardian-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup> Javier Correa, the president of Sinaltrainal, said the campaign aimed to put pressure on Coca-Cola "to mitigate the pain and suffering" that union members had suffered.<sup id="cite_ref-Guardian_142-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Guardian-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Speaking from the Coca-Cola company's headquarters in Atlanta, company spokesperson Rafael Fernandez Quiros said "Coca-Cola denies any connection to any human-rights violation of this type" and added "We do not own or operate the plants".<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A documentary on the controversy, titled <i>The Coca-Cola Case</i>, was released in 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Use_as_political_and_corporate_symbol">Use as political and corporate symbol</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:102px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Coca-Cola_2.5L_bottle_in_Mainland_China_20160514.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Coca-Cola_2.5L_bottle_in_Mainland_China_20160514.jpg/100px-Coca-Cola_2.5L_bottle_in_Mainland_China_20160514.jpg" width="100" height="301" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Coca-Cola_2.5L_bottle_in_Mainland_China_20160514.jpg/150px-Coca-Cola_2.5L_bottle_in_Mainland_China_20160514.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Coca-Cola_2.5L_bottle_in_Mainland_China_20160514.jpg/200px-Coca-Cola_2.5L_bottle_in_Mainland_China_20160514.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1131" data-file-height="3399" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Coca-Cola_2.5L_bottle_in_Mainland_China_20160514.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>As sold in China</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:NASA_FGBA.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/NASA_FGBA.JPG/200px-NASA_FGBA.JPG" width="200" height="288" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/NASA_FGBA.JPG/300px-NASA_FGBA.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/NASA_FGBA.JPG/400px-NASA_FGBA.JPG 2x" data-file-width="417" data-file-height="600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:NASA_FGBA.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Astronauts served Coca-Cola from this device on the <a href="/wiki/STS-63" title="STS-63">Space Shuttle</a> in 1995.</div></div></div>
<p>Coca-Cola has a high degree of identification with the United States, being considered by some an "American Brand" or as an item representing America. During <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, this gave rise to brief production of the <a href="/wiki/White_Coke" title="White Coke">White Coke</a> as a neutral brand.<sup id="cite_ref-NYT_145-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup> The drink is also often a <a href="/wiki/Metonymy" title="Metonymy">metonym</a> for the Coca-Cola Company.
</p><p>Coca-Cola was introduced to China in 1927, and was very popular until 1949. After the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_Civil_War" title="Chinese Civil War">Chinese Civil War</a> ended in 1949, the beverage was no longer imported into China, as it was perceived to be a symbol of decadent <a href="/wiki/Western_culture" title="Western culture">Western culture</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Capitalism" title="Capitalism">capitalist</a> lifestyle. Importation and sales of the beverage resumed in 1979, after diplomatic relations between the United States and China were restored.<sup id="cite_ref-weibo_146-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-weibo-146">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>There are some consumer boycotts of Coca-Cola in <a href="/wiki/Arab_world" title="Arab world">Arab countries</a> due to Coke's early investment in Israel during the <a href="/wiki/Arab_League_boycott_of_Israel" title="Arab League boycott of Israel">Arab League boycott of Israel</a> (its competitor Pepsi stayed out of Israel).<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup>  <a href="/wiki/Mecca_Cola" class="mw-redirect" title="Mecca Cola">Mecca Cola</a> and Pepsi have been successful<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Vagueness" title="Wikipedia:Vagueness"><span title="This information is too vague. (February 2012)">vague</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> alternatives in the Middle East.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A Coca-Cola fountain dispenser (officially a Fluids Generic Bioprocessing Apparatus or FGBA) was developed for use on the <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle" title="Space Shuttle">Space Shuttle</a> as a test bed to determine if carbonated beverages can be produced from separately stored carbon dioxide, water, and flavored syrups and determine if the resulting fluids can be made available for consumption without bubble nucleation and resulting foam formation. FGBA-1 flew on <a href="/wiki/STS-63" title="STS-63">STS-63</a> in 1995 and dispensed pre-mixed beverages, followed by FGBA-2 on <a href="/wiki/STS-77" title="STS-77">STS-77</a> the next year. The latter mixed CO₂, water, and syrup to make beverages.  It supplied 1.65&#160;liters each of Coca-Cola and Diet Coke.<sup id="cite_ref-CokeInSpace_149-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CokeInSpace-149">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<li id="cite_note-mayclifford-67"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-mayclifford_67-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-mayclifford_67-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">May, Clifford D. <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.nytimes.com/1988/07/01/business/how-coca-cola-obtains-its-coca.html">"How Coca-Cola Obtains Its Coca"</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i>, July 1, 1988. Accessed April 11, 2008. "A Stepan laboratory in Maywood, N.J., is the nation's only legal commercial importer of coca leaves, which it obtains mainly from Peru and, to a lesser extent, Bolivia. Besides producing the coca flavoring agent sold to The Coca-Cola Company, Stepan extracts <a href="/wiki/Cocaine" title="Cocaine">cocaine</a> from the coca leaves, which it sells to <a href="/wiki/Mallinckrodt" title="Mallinckrodt">Mallinckrodt</a> Inc., a St. Louis pharmaceutical manufacturer that is the only company in the United States licensed to purify the product for medicinal use." See links for more information</span>
